OSC 133 shell integration sequences (exact boundaries + exit code)\n6\t * 2. Quiet-period detection (500ms silence → idle)\n7\t *\n8\t * Emits: command_start, command_end, idle\n9\t */\n10\timport { EventEmitter } from \"events\";\n11\timport { stripAnsi } from \"../types.js\";\n12\t\n13\texport type ShellState = \"idle\" | \"running\";\n14\texport type DetectionMethod = \"osc133\" | \"quiet\";\n15\t\n16\texport interface CommandEvent {\n17\t  command: string;\n18\t  exitCode?: number;\n19\t  output?: string;\n20\t  startedAt: string;\n21\t  completedAt?: string;\n22\t  durationMs?: number;\n23\t  cwd?: string;\n24\t  detectionMethod: DetectionMethod;\n25\t}\n26\t\n27\t// OSC 133 sequences (used by iTerm2 shell integration, Kitty, etc.)\n28\t// A = prompt start, B = prompt end, C = command start, D = command end (with exit code)\n29\tconst OSC133_A = /\\x1b\\]133;A/;  // Prompt start\n30\tconst OSC133_C = /\\x1b\\]133;C/;  // Command start (user pressed Enter)\n31\tconst OSC133_D = /\\x1b\\]133;D(?:;(\\d+))?/;  // Command end, optional exit code\n32\t\n33\t// OSC 133 B + preexec: some shells emit the command text\n34\tconst OSC133_B = /\\x1b\\]133;B/;  // Command line ready\n35\t\n36\tconst QUIET_TIMEOUT_MS = 500;\n37\t\n38\texport class CommandTracker extends EventEmitter {\n39\t  private shellState: ShellState = \"idle\";\n40\t  private currentCommand: string | null = null;\n41\t  private commandStartedAt: string | null = null;\n42\t  private outputBuffer: string[] = [];\n43\t  private quietTimer: ReturnType<typeof setTimeout> | null = null;\n44\t  private hasOsc133 = false;\n45\t  private lastPromptLine: string | null = null;\n46\t\n47\t  get state(): ShellState {\n48\t    return this.shellState;\n49\t  }\n50\t\n51\t  get usingOsc133(): boolean {\n52\t    return this.hasOsc133;\n53\t  }\n54\t\n55\t  /**\n56\t   * Feed raw terminal output into the tracker.\n57\t   * Call this for every chunk from the PTY.\n58\t   */\n59\t  feed(data: string): void {\n60\t    // Check for OSC 133 sequences\n61\t    if (this.checkOsc133(data)) return;\n62\t\n63\t    // Quiet-period fallback: reset timer on every output chunk\n64\t    if (this.shellState === \"running\") {\n65\t      this.outputBuffer.push(data);\n66\t      this.resetQuietTimer();\n67\t    }\n68\t  }\n69\t\n70\t  /**\n71\t   * Notify the tracker that input was sent to the terminal.\n72\t   * Used to detect command starts in quiet-period mode.\n73\t   */\n74\t  inputSent(data: string): void {\n75\t    if (this.hasOsc133) return; // OSC 133 handles this\n76\t\n77\t    // If Enter was pressed and we're idle, start a command\n78\t    if ((data.includes(\"\\r\") || data.includes(\"\\n\")) && this.shellState === \"idle\") {\n79\t      this.shellState = \"running\";\n80\t      this.commandStartedAt = new Date().toISOString();\n81\t      this.currentCommand = data.trim() || \"(unknown)\";\n82\t      this.outputBuffer = [];\n83\t      this.emit(\"command_start\", {\n84\t        command: this.currentCommand,\n85\t        startedAt: this.commandStartedAt,\n86\t        detectionMethod: \"quiet\" as DetectionMethod,\n87\t      });\n88\t      this.resetQuietTimer();\n89\t    }\n90\t  }\n91\t\n92\t  private checkOsc133(data: string): boolean {\n93\t    let handled = false;\n94\t\n95\t    // Check for prompt start (A) — indicates shell is idle/ready\n96\t    if (OSC133_A.test(data)) {\n97\t      this.hasOsc133 = true;\n98\t      handled = true;\n99\t\n100\t      if (this.shellState === \"running\" && this.commandStartedAt) {\n101\t        // Command ended (prompt returned)\n102\t        this.completeCommand(undefined, \"osc133\");\n103\t      }\n104\t      this.shellState = \"idle\";\n105\t      this.cancelQuietTimer();\n106\t      this.emit(\"idle\");\n107\t    }\n108\t\n109\t    // Check for command start (C) — user pressed Enter\n110\t    if (OSC133_C.test(data)) {\n111\t      this.hasOsc133 = true;\n112\t      handled = true;\n113\t\n114\t      this.shellState = \"running\";\n115\t      this.commandStartedAt = new Date().toISOString();\n116\t      this.outputBuffer = [];\n117\t\n118\t      // Try to extract the command from the prompt line\n119\t      const plain = stripAnsi(data);\n120\t      // Command text is typically between B and C markers\n121\t      this.currentCommand = this.lastPromptLine || plain.trim() || \"(unknown)\";\n122\t\n123\t      this.emit(\"command_start\", {\n124\t        command: this.currentCommand,\n125\t        startedAt: this.commandStartedAt,\n126\t        detectionMethod: \"osc133\" as DetectionMethod,\n127\t      });\n128\t    }\n129\t\n130\t    // Check for prompt line ready (B) — capture the command text\n131\t    if (OSC133_B.test(data)) {\n132\t      this.hasOsc133 = true;\n133\t      // The text between A and B markers is the prompt; text after B is the command\n134\t      const plain = stripAnsi(data);\n135\t      const parts = plain.split(/\\x1b\\]133;B/);\n136\t      this.lastPromptLine = parts.length > 1 ? parts[1].trim() : plain.trim();\n137\t    }\n138\t\n139\t    // Check for command end (D) — with optional exit Pexpect is pure Python and doesn't require TCL or Expect nor does it require C extensions to be compiled. It should work on any platform that supports the standard Python pty module.\n\nThis works even for commands that ask for passwords or other input outside of the normal stdio streams. This is possible because Pexpect operates through PTYs (pseudo-terminals), which simulate a real terminal environment.\n\n## Tmux send-keys Operation\n\nThe `send-keys` command programmatically sends keystrokes to a target pane. Send keys work by simulating keystrokes in a terminal. They translate typed instructions into virtual keystrokes that can be used to execute commands.\n\nSend keys refers to a series of keystrokes that serve as an automated instruction to one of the terminal panes in the tmux window. By using send keys, you can automate certain processes in tmux panes.\n\nThe key difference is that tmux's send-keys operates on tmux panes (which are already running within tmux's own PTY infrastructure), whereas pexpect directly manages spawning child processes in their own PTYs. Both ultimately rely on the underlying PTY mechanism for delivering input as if it were typed interactively.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"5fcd124e-0b04-4520-a835-d25dcadd9217","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:44:32.499Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"28bc6110-8dde-45f1-a01a-8922c159ac37","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Every command execution produces a block: a discrete container holding the command, its output, and metadata (execution time, exit code, timestamp).\n\nBlocks transform the terminal from a continuous scroll into a structured document. This represents a fundamental shift in how terminal interfaces work, allowing developers to interact with command results as organized, discrete units rather than continuous text streams.\n\n## Structured Output Features\n\nThe block-based architecture enables several key capabilities:\n\n- You can jump between blocks, search inside them, copy only the output, or even share just one block with a teammate.\n- Warp stores structured command history across sessions. Many of the newer terminals support OSC 133 for semantic markers so the shell can advise them of where prompts, right prompts, input areas and output are, which enables features such as jumping to prompts or selecting the output from a single command.\n\n## Sequence Types\n\nThe OSC 133 protocol includes: OSC 133 ; A ST (Mark prompt start), OSC 133 ; B ST (Mark prompt end), OSC 133 ; C ST (Mark pre-execution), and OSC 133 ; D [; <exitcode>] ST (Mark execution finished with an optional exit code).\n\n## Recent Terminal Support (2025-2026)\n\nTerminals like Ghostty (1.3+), iTerm2, WezTerm, and others support OSC 133 to understand prompt boundaries. A new feature in recent versions is the ability to position the cursor by clicking in shell prompts, which utilizes OSC 133 extensions click-events and cl=line.\n\nGhostty implements OSC 133 support across five shells: Bash, Zsh, Fish, Elvish, and Nushell. The developers have revised their OSC 133 implementation and now rely on region-based detection, which works more accurately than the row-based variant used by some competitors.\n\n## Shell Integration Implementation\n\nMany of the newer terminals support OSC 133 for semantic markers, and recent patches such as the Zsh implementation from March 2025 add direct support for these semantic markers in shell configurations.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"69193576-1b55-4a52-9d52-5996eba2c527","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:44:34.756Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5b550cb1-4829-4dc4-ac35-8bf81948cd9d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Unlike other crates, it provides a set of traits that allow selecting from different implementations at runtime, and it is part of wezterm.\n\n## Node-pty Alternatives\n\nSeveral projects leverage portable-pty as an alternative to node-pty:\n\n- **bun-pty**: A cross-platform pseudo-terminal implementation for Bun, powered by Rust's portable-pty library and Bun's FFI capabilities.\n\n- **get-pty-output**: This package leverages Rust's portable-pty (unix) and conpty (windows) crates with n-api-rs for bindings, allowing way better cross-platform support and easier installation. Unlike node-pty which relies on node-gyp, it supports newer Node versions.\n\nThese Rust-based alternatives represent a modern approach to handling PTY functionality that addresses some of the limitations and maintenance issues with node-pty.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"2e31cd61-58fe-4bfb-a541-387f75337965","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:44:44.111Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c6fdf918-ee08-4ba4-9dce-72c6ff698868","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Terminal emulators like xterm and iTerm2 allow programs to configure the terminal emulator's behavior via escape codes, which programs typically do by printing a control sequence to standard out.\n\n**Purpose and Benefits:**\nPrograms can tell the difference between stuff you type manually and stuff you pasted. Bracketed paste mode is explicitly designed to neutralize paste attacks, with terminals wrapping pasted text in special escape sequences to inform the shell of that text's origin, allowing the shell to ignore special editing characters found in the pasted text.\n\n**Implementation:**\nTo enable bracketed paste mode you need to output the sequence \\e[?2004h to STDOUT when your program starts, and output the sequence \\e[?2004l to STDOUT to turn it off.\n\n**PTY and Terminal Support:**\nTo use bracketed paste mode, both the connecting user's terminal emulator and the target machine's shell or application must be aware of the feature. xterm, urxvt, iTerm2, gnome-terminal (and other terminals using libvte), and Putty (for Windows) are known to work.\n\n**Security Considerations:**\nIf data to paste contains ^C, then a SIGINT will be sent to the shell and data from the start of the paste up to ^C will be discarded by the line discipline, which relates to raw mode input handling in PTY contexts.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"50487132-8d04-4788-a63e-c3e603cfe90c","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:44:46.228Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d1404470-322b-4d9a-9b37-5409970c34f9","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

This makes asciicast files significantly more efficient than traditional video recording formats.\n\n## Format Structure\n\nThe first line of the file, encoded as a JSON object, represents the header containing metadata such as initial terminal size and timestamp, while all following lines form the event stream with each line representing a separate event encoded as a 3-element JSON array.\n\n## Recent Developments\n\nThe new asciicast v3 format is the default output format in recent versions, using relative timestamps (intervals) for events while v2 used absolute time (since session start) - making edits substantially easier.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"28b620b1-d962-4d0a-bd93-8c2e9232bc7c","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:44:47.653Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"1ae0cf7f-3147-46aa-bd5b-90f8dc94bbf2","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

I found the Warp terminal blog post about data structures. Here's what I found:\n\n## Key Findings\n\nGrids - two-dimensional arrays of characters - are the universal building blocks of terminals, and basic terminal operations like entering a command, receiving output, and scrolling through a file are operations on grids.\n\nIn Warp, commands are separated into blocks, and each block consists of three grids: one for the prompt, one for the command input, and one for the command output.\n\nWarp's grid is a circular buffer that stores rows sequentially in a vector. This architectural choice represents one of the key performance trade-offs in terminal design, as Warp's implementation of the grid is an opinionated stance on which terminal operations should be most efficient at, with the reality being that optimizing for one almost always means not optimizing for another.\n\nThe blog post explores the performance considerations behind terminal grids and how different design decisions affect operations like reading data from the shell, scrolling through content, and resizing screens or splitting panes.\n\nAdditionally, regarding the broader Warp architecture, given the lack of stable UI framework options for Rust, the team decided to build their own UI framework in Rust, which essentially amounted to building the architecture of a browser.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"ba177efe-d94d-4566-ac19-a79432e59d77","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:44:58.963Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"7ad89f23-c638-49cc-a9a9-865ad7ebf3c1","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Originally created for Mux (a desktop app for isolated, parallel agentic development), but designed to be used anywhere.\n\n## Key Advantages Over xterm.js\n\nThe project addresses fundamental limitations of xterm.js. While xterm.js reimplements terminal emulation in JavaScript with every escape sequence and edge case hand-coded, Ghostty's emulator is the same battle-tested code that runs the native Ghostty app.\n\n## Technical Details\n\nThe project features xterm.js API Compatibility as a drop-in replacement with minimal migration effort (change import statement only), uses Ghostty's VT100 parser (written in Zig) compiled to WASM, and has a ~400KB WASM bundle with no runtime dependencies.\n\n## Migration Path\n\nYou can migrate from xterm by changing your import from @xterm/xterm to ghostty-web.\n\n## Related Projects\n\nvscode-bootty is an alternative terminal extension for VS Code powered by Ghostty's terminal emulation (libghostty-vt) via WebAssembly.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"934f205f-e089-4ffb-8f2c-ad91cf5a7155","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:11.755Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"dc6101c5-c192-4ad5-b894-8e22dca59173","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Here's what I found:\n\n## PTY Pseudo-Terminal Basics\n\nData written on the master end is provided to the process on the slave end as though it was input typed on a terminal. Data flow between master and slave is handled asynchronously, much like data flow with a physical terminal.\n\n## Simulating Typing with Natural Delays\n\nThe search results reveal two approaches to simulating natural typing delays:\n\n**Character-by-Character Approach:**\nThe program basically did this the following for each character in a given string: Insert a delay. Print the character. Flush the output buffer so it shows up on screen. This approach allows you to add different delays for different character types (letters, spaces, newlines).\n\n**Shell Loop Alternative:**\nThis line reads characters one at a time from standard input, preserving whitespace, then prints the character stored in the char variable with a delay of 0.1 seconds after printing each character.\n\n## Key Consideration\n\nData flow between master and slave is handled asynchronously, much like data flow with a physical terminal. Data written to the slave will be available at the master promptly, but may not be available immediately. This means that when writing to a PTY, there's inherent asynchronous behavior to consider when implementing character-by-character delays.\n\nThe search results don't contain a direct technical comparison of these approaches for PTY input specifically, so you may want to consult PTY-specific documentation or networking/terminal automation resources for more detailed implementation guidance.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"2c3a79f4-447a-4620-837a-64774e3f9c0c","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:22.928Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"34435955-2b84-42d6-b534-cbe05a16d4ad","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Cursor itself is a graphical IDE, yet its agentic capabilities operate through a terminal-like text channel embedded within the editor.\n\nThe paper makes an important point about design philosophy: Rather than a legacy artifact, the terminal serves as a design exemplar whose properties any agent-facing modality must replicate.\n\n**Paper Details:**\n- **ArXiv ID:** 2603.10664\n- **Author:** Alexandre De Masi\n- **Submission Date:** March 11, 2026\n\nThis research represents a significant shift in how we think about AI agent interface design, emphasizing that terminal-based interactions may be superior not due to tradition, but due to fundamental design principles that support human-AI collaboration.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"a2b61dec-4bde-4da8-945c-afc2432c92df","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:24.066Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"3c7eac87-d011-4b07-a139-9228e378e95f","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

It's a very thin wrapper around PTYs and processes.\n\n## Architecture\n\nThe project uses napi (version 2.12.2 with napi4 feature enabled) as its primary dependency for Node-API integration, along with libc, nix, and other supporting libraries. The codebase is written in Rust and compiles to a native C dynamic library (cdylib).\n\n## How It Works\n\nThe library exposes a Pty class to JavaScript that allows you to create and interact with pseudo-terminals. You can instantiate a Pty with command, args, environment variables, and size configuration, then read data from and write to the PTY using event-based streams.\n\n## Comparison to node-pty\n\nRuspty was originally created instead of using node-pty because early versions of Replit's pid2 used Bun, which didn't have great support for Node-API native addons at the time. However, even after migrating back to Node.js, they kept ruspty to retain access to system-call level details. \n\nHaving deep granularity into system-call level details allows for flexibility—for example, adding non-interactive mode to PTYs, which node-pty doesn't support.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"334e9e91-8992-46b1-81ed-74b8ab52847c","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:24.914Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"a6181e57-a198-44fd-9b01-edfc24e248e0","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

This change greatly simplifies the editing process of recording files. When adding, deleting, or modifying events, there is no longer a need to adjust the timestamps of all subsequent events, greatly improving the maintainability of recording files.\n\n**Header Structure:** The v3 format has optimized the header structure, grouping related information. For example, all terminal-related metadata is grouped under term.\n\n**New Event Types:** It also adds a new \"x\" (exit) event type to record the session's exit status.\n\n**Format Features:** By default, asciinema rec saves a session as an asciicast v3-formatted file, which is a newline-delimited JSON format. To enhance readability, the v3 format also allows the use of # as the first character of a line to add inline comments.\n\n**Compatibility:** asciicast v3 file format is not backward compatible with asciicast v1/v2 due to the header schema changes and use of relative time (intervals) in the event stream.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"3a33774b-a785-43c0-bbe9-2049fd4be314","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:25.801Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"cfeb0311-e915-42e8-9705-8b2b7da20422","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

When a program interacts with the pty, it communicates through the master file descriptor and while interacting with tty, it uses slave file descriptor.\n\n## Data Flow Between Master and Slave\n\nWhen you write to one file, the data is available to be read from the other. More specifically:\n\n- Anything that is written on the master end is provided to the process on the slave end as though it was input typed on a terminal.\n- Anything that is written to the slave end of the pseudoterminal can be read by the process that is connected to the master end.\n\n## How Expect Works with PTYs\n\nThe spawn functions uses a pty to communicate with the process. By default, the pty is initialized the same way as the user's tty (if possible, i.e., if the environment has a controlling terminal.)\n\nThe stream versions of the expect functions are much slower than the file descriptor versions because there is no way to portably read an unknown number of bytes without the potential of timing out.\n\n## PTY Trapping and Control\n\nPty trapping can be controlled with exp_slave_control. The first argument is the file descriptor corresponding to the spawned process. The second argument is a 0 if trapping is to be disabled and 1 if it is to be enabled.\n\nInternally, expect calls read() which can be interrupted by signals.\n\n## Reading Operations\n\nThe functions master_read and stdin_read are passed a file descriptor which they should read from, and they should always return a byte string.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"9682db37-b537-4bee-bb72-8712b34d6289","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:38.154Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"fbbfc4f9-8b4a-4ed4-901d-38c9d09fc86d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Here's a summary of the key information:\n\n## Paper Overview\n\nThe landscape of AI coding assistance is undergoing a fundamental shift from complex IDE plugins to versatile, terminal-native agents, with CLI-based agents offering unprecedented autonomy for long-horizon development tasks.\n\n## OPENDEV System\n\nThe paper presents OPENDEV, an open-source, command-line coding agent written in Rust, engineered specifically for this new paradigm.\n\n## Key Technical Approach\n\nEffective autonomous assistance requires strict safety controls and highly efficient context management to prevent context bloat and reasoning degradation, which OPENDEV overcomes through a compound AI system architecture with workload-specialized model routing, a dual-agent architecture separating planning from execution, lazy tool discovery, and adaptive context compaction that progressively reduces older observations.\n\n## Scaffolding and Harness Architecture\n\nThe paper discusses two central concepts:\n\n- **Scaffolding**: Scaffolding is concerned with constructing the agent before the first prompt, involving how agents are assembled.\n\n- **Harness**: The harness is concerned with everything that happens after: dispatching tools, compacting context, enforcing safety invariants, and persisting state across turns.\n\nThe paper (arxiv ID: 2603.05344) was published approximately 3 Additionally, each window has a backlog for scrolled off lines which can be accessed for copying, too.\n\n## Architecture for Concurrent Session Management\n\nA high-level architecture includes: one terminal multiplexer process managing multiple concurrent sessions, per-session isolation so each agent/task can run independently, a persistent sidebar that tracks session state/activity without switching context.\n\nTmux operates on a client-server model. When you start tmux, it creates a server process that manages all your sessions. The tmux client connects to this server to interact with your sessions. This architecture is what enables the detach/reattach functionality that makes tmux so powerful.\n\n## Resource Efficiency\n\nResource management benefits include: if you're working on a constrained machine, having only a terminal open a multiplexer in it is easier on CPU/RAM consumption rather than multiple windows or tabs.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"4e733fa9-e0cf-4522-a640-2523e6ea05e0","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:55.770Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d11093e0-77fe-41d0-b9ba-11c23c72c86a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

we're idle, start a command\n78\t    if ((data.includes(\"\\r\") || data.includes(\"\\n\")) && this.shellState === \"idle\") {\n79\t      this.shellState = \"running\";\n80\t      this.commandStartedAt = new Date().toISOString();\n81\t      this.currentCommand = data.trim() || \"(unknown)\";\n82\t      this.outputBuffer = [];\n83\t      this.emit(\"command_start\", {\n84\t        command: this.currentCommand,\n85\t        startedAt: this.commandStartedAt,\n86\t        detectionMethod: \"quiet\" as DetectionMethod,\n87\t      });\n88\t      this.resetQuietTimer();\n89\t    }\n90\t  }\n91\t\n92\t  private checkOsc133(data: string): boolean {\n93\t    let handled = false;\n94\t\n95\t    // Check for prompt start (A) — indicates shell is idle/ready\n96\t    if (OSC133_A.test(data)) {\n97\t      this.hasOsc133 = true;\n98\t      handled = true;\n99\t\n100\t      if (this.shellState === \"running\" && this.commandStartedAt) {\n101\t        // Command ended (prompt returned)\n102\t        this.completeCommand(undefined, \"osc133\");\n103\t      }\n104\t      this.shellState = \"idle\";\n105\t      this.cancelQuietTimer();\n106\t      this.emit(\"idle\");\n107\t    }\n108\t\n109\t    // Check for command start (C) — user pressed Enter\n110\t    if (OSC133_C.test(data)) {\n111\t      this.hasOsc133 = true;\n112\t      handled = The spawn class is the more powerful interface to the Pexpect system, allowing you to spawn a child program then interact with it by sending input and expecting responses (waiting for patterns in the child's output).\n\nPexpect is in the spirit of Don Libes' Expect, but Pexpect is pure Python. Unlike other Expect-like modules for Python, Pexpect does not require TCL or Expect nor does it require C extensions to be compiled. It should work on any platform that supports the standard Python pty module.\n\n## Send Operations\n\nThe send() method writes a string to the child application. From the child's point of view it looks just like someone typed the text from a terminal.\n\n## TMux send-keys\n\nTo send key presses to the running program you can use \"tmux -L $ID send-key <some-char>\". These can either be ascii characters or a variety of control ones.\n\n## PTY Line Handling\n\nAn important technical detail about PTY behavior: Each line of text ends with a CR/LF combination. When you intercept data from a UNIX command from a TTY device you will find that the TTY device outputs a CR/LF combination. A UNIX command may only write a linefeed (\\n), but the TTY device driver converts it to CR/LF. This means that your terminal will see lines end with CR/LF (hex 0D 0A).\n\nThe search results don't contain specific documentation from 2025-2026 about these tools, as they cover stable features that haven't changed recently.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"26494a32-1c9d-4602-af9f-b2a6d2ea0110","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:46:32.750Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"58025576-1a5b-4d75-9b82-253eacf6742d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

It is a control sequence that specifies where the prompt ended, and where the output of the executed program starts and ends.\n\n## Key Terminal Support\n\nTerminals like Ghostty (1.3+), iTerm2, WezTerm, and others support OSC 133 to understand prompt boundaries. Ghostty implements OSC 133 support across five shells: Bash, Zsh, Fish, Elvish, and Nushell.\n\n## Primary Sequence Types\n\nThe protocol defines four main sequences: OSC 133;A (the start of a prompt), OSC 133;B (the start of a commandline/end of prompt), OSC 133;C (the start of command output/end of commandline), and OSC 133;D (the end of a command).\n\n## Features Enabled\n\nWhen CLI applications emit these sequences, users get click-to-move cursor, jump-to-prompt keyboard shortcuts, and the ability to select command output. This enables features such as jumping to prompts or selecting the output from a single command.\n\n## Recent Developments\n\nGhostty has revised its OSC 133 implementation and now relies on region-based detection, which works more accurately than the row-based variant used by some competitors. When you execute a command, your command and output are grouped into a Block, which you can jump between, search inside them, copy only the output, or even share just one block with a teammate.\n\n## Grid-Based Storage System\n\nThe underlying storage mechanism uses grids. If the shell originally outputs a grid that is 10 rows by 10 columns and the user proceeds to resize the screen that makes the grid 5 columns, then internally the vector is re-written to account for this new horizontal constraint: the grid becomes 20 rows by 5 columns both on screen and in storage.\n\nFor efficient scrolling, the grid stores two integers that allow us to efficiently translate between screen space and vector space. This optimization allows Warp to complete scroll operations without moving any data in the vector.\n\n## Modern Implementation\n\nThis is implemented in Rust and involves a unique UI framework that eschews traditional terminal emulation libraries. The terminal's advanced input editor functions as a full-featured text editor, allowing users to interact with command output through grouped \"blocks\" and layered UI components for enhanced navigation and functionality.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"c2715ab4-8d69-4c13-8aae-40171ee5218c","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:46:34.632Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d4605f7d-250d-4ad6-8d13-b690a3bf79e0","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

The metadata captured includes:\n\nWorking directory, exit code, duration, and hostname, along with timestamp, command duration, exit code of the command, the command itself, current working directory, and session ID associated with a terminal session.\n\nAtuin features advanced search modes including session, directory, and global search, command duration tracking, stats and analytics, better filtering for specific exit codes and time ranges, session tracking, and SQLite backend with richer metadata.\n\n## McFly's Approach\n\nMcFly uses neural networks to rank history based on context, tracks exit status and command sequences, and provides context-aware suggestions.\n\n## Key Architectural Differences\n\nAtuin handles multiline commands correctly where McFly splits them, with advanced filtering and stats as additional benefits. \n\nAdditionally, there is integration available that captures shell commands executed by Claude Code and adds them to Atuin history database with proper directory context, using Claude Code's PostToolUse hook system.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d362b416-3a19-4331-b8ab-cedf3d14cef9","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:46:46.081Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"f807fb5c-f794-4f0b-926a-1d33385af275","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Here's what the search revealed:\n\n## Bracketed Paste Mode Overview\n\nWhen you are in bracketed paste mode and you paste into your terminal the content will be wrapped by the sequences \\e[200~ and \\e[201~. Programs can tell the difference between stuff you type manually and stuff you pasted.\n\n## Enabling and Disabling\n\nTo enable it you need to output the sequence \\e[?2004h to STDOUT when your program starts. As bracketed paste mode is global to the terminal window you need to remember to turn it off again when your program exits. To do this output the sequence \\e[?2004l to STDOUT.\n\n## Security Concerns and Control Character Handling\n\nIf the data to paste (which is written in one go to the master side of the pty by the terminal emulator) contains ^C, then a SIGINT will be sent to zsh and the data from the start of the paste up to ^C will not be available for reading (discarded by the line discipline, at least on Linux).\n\nxterm handles bracketed paste mode by wrapping pasted text with the control sequences \\e[200~ and \\e[201~ and sending it to the PTY. The default disallowedPasteControls includes STTY, meaning if the PTY's VINTR is set to 0x03 (Ctrl+C), that character will be filtered out during a paste to prevent accidental signal generation.\n\n## Raw Mode PTY Handling\n\nThe root cause appears to be Cursor's bundled node-pty 1.1.0-beta42 failing to handle backpressure when large paste input is sent to a raw-mode PTY.\n\n## Practical Benefits\n\nModern versions of bash will highlight pasted data on the command line, and not run it until you've confirmed it by pressing Return, even if the pasted data contained a newline character.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"98cecc91-f7e8-48bd-8e68-a8d7d2495d9c","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:46:49.180Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c7d4c1a5-a947-4d98-ba6b-2a6518b34da2","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Here are the key findings:\n\n## Time-Series Database Architecture & Storage Efficiency\n\nTime-series databases (TSDBs) are specialized storage systems optimized for data points indexed by time, built from the ground up with time as their primary organizing principle.\n\n### Storage Optimization\n\nTime-series databases incorporate features such as data compression to reduce storage costs, time-based indexing for faster querying, and built-in support for time-specific calculations. TSDBs typically use columnar storage, and this enables dramatic compression—sequential time-series values often change gradually, allowing delta encoding and other specialized compression techniques to achieve 10-100x compression ratios.\n\n### Schema Design\n\nData points have essential components: metric_name with tags and values plus timestamps, allowing for efficient querying across different dimensions. InfluxDB has a line protocol for sending time series data which takes the form: measurement-name tag-set field-set timestamp.\n\n### Efficient Search Capabilities\n\nLSM trees are the secret sauce behind many high-write-throughput databases, including InfluxDB, Cassandra, and LevelDB, transforming expensive random writes into cheap sequential writes, then periodically reorganizing data in the background to make reads more efficient.\n\n### 2025 Database Solutions\n\nRecent 2025 resources highlight advanced implementations: Time-series databases are much more efficient in terms of ingestion rate, query latency, and storage costs compared to general-purpose databases.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"77804f0a-f2ae-4eb4-8a8b-4d09574f4b39","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:47:01.389Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"fcb786ea-0d3a-4eee-8afb-51fa2fd9045c","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Here are the key findings:\n\n## Overview\n\nTIOCSTI is an ioctl syscall command in the Linux kernel that allows a process to simulate terminal input by injecting characters into the input queue of a terminal device as if they were typed by a user.\n\n## Deprecation Status\n\nLinux kernel >= 6.2.0 disabled TIOCSTI by default in several distributions. It is possible to disable it since 2022, Kernel version 6.2.\n\n## Security Concerns\n\nThe TIOCSTI ioctl presents significant security risks. An attacker can use TIOCSTI to inject commands into another user's terminal session, especially if the attacker has access to the same terminal device (e.g., /dev/pts/X). A malicious program run under sudo may be capable of injecting commands into the user's terminal or running a background process that retains access to the user's terminal device even after the main program has finished executing. By running the command in a separate pseudo-terminal, this attack is no longer possible.\n\n## Recommended Alternatives\n\nAlan Cox, who maintained the TTY subsystem, stated in 2017 that processes with that problem should create a new pseudoterminal and use that: \"The proper way to handle those cases is to create a pty/tty pair and use that.\" The pseudo terminal approach has been successfully used by su (with the --pty flag) and sudo.\n\nSimulation of the terminal input by injecting characters into the input queue of a terminal device without TIOCSTI by writing to /dev/tty device is also mentioned as an alternative approach.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"a3c220ce-bca5-4c79-bbd0-7c8cd7e5e2fe","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:47:14.609Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5cffc3eb-eb9a-4059-b4e6-edbf0c10db9a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Key header fields include:\n\n- Terminal type, e.g. xterm-256color\n- Terminal version as reported by XTVERSION OSC query\n- Color theme of the recorded terminal (an object with the following attributes, all required)\n- Unix timestamp of the beginning of the recording session\n- Idle time limit, which should be used by an asciicast player to reduce all terminal inactivity to a maximum of idle_time_limit value\n\n## Event Codes\n\nEvent codes include \"o\" for output, \"i\" for input, \"m\" for a marker used for playback or navigation, and so forth. Additional event types include:\n\n- Event with code \"m\" represents a marker that can act as breakpoints or be used for playback navigation and automation\n- Event with code \"r\" represents terminal resize, with data containing new terminal size (columns + rows) formatted as \"{COLS}x{ROWS}\"\n- Event with code \"x\" represents session exit status and is typically included only once, as the very last event of the recording\n\n## Key Format Changes\n\nThe v3 format uses event deltas to record timestamps for session events, rather than absolute timestamps as in v2, which greatly simplifies the editing process of recording files.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"7946d620-e788-4959-b3f8-8e9c678db52f","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:47:15.268Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"b3e1dd63-005e-479a-a4ea-bb88332fb8f0","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

When using this tool, you don't need to provide an input schema as with other tools; the schema is built into Claude's model and can't be modified.\n\nThe implementation uses Python's subprocess module. The standard implementation creates a BashSession using subprocess.Popen to spawn /bin/bash with stdin, stdout, and stderr pipes, manages output queues, and sends commands via stdin.write() with flushing, capturing output with timeouts.\n\n### Current Limitations with PTY\n\nClaude Code currently does **not** use pseudo-terminals (PTY) for bash command execution. Claude Code executes bash commands in a subprocess and captures stdout. Terminal escape sequences (OSC codes) require direct TTY access to be interpreted by the terminal emulator.\n\n### PTY Feature Requests\n\nA proposal exists to enhance the Bash tool to support fully interactive shell commands by integrating a pseudo-terminal (PTY). This would allow Claude Code to spawn and manage interactive subprocesses, rendering their UI directly within the Claude Code REPL. This design makes scrolling operations efficient, though it comes with tradeoffs in other areas.\n\n## Rust Implementation Foundation\n\nWarp started by forking Alacritty's model code, which was well suited for it because it was already written in Rust and is tailored to be as performant as possible.\n\n## Command Block Architecture\n\nWarp has a distinctive architecture for handling command blocks that differs from traditional terminal design. Warp separates commands into blocks, and each block consists of three grids: one for the prompt, one for the command input, and one for the command output.\n\nThe reason for this multi-grid approach is important: A single grid would not work for building a feature like blocks: separating commands from each other requires ensuring that terminal output is actually written on separate lines of a grid and can't be overwritten by output from another command. To fix these issues, they create a separate grid for each command and output based on the precmd/preexec hooks they receive from the shell.\n\n## Internal Architecture Trade-offs\n\nWarp's implementation of the grid is itself an opinionated stance on which of these operations a terminal should be most efficient at, with the reality being that optimizing for one almost always means not optimizing for another.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d3e191a0-5945-44bf-bddb-f82d4bbba3d6","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:47:24.883Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"1734ecbf-6cc4-447a-ace5-75bfb238ded7","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

You can then send commands to the shell by writing to the master end and receive the output of the commands by reading from it.\n\n**node-pty's write() method:**\nnode-pty provides a `write()` method (e.g., `ptyProcess.write('ls\\r')`) that sends input to the spawned shell process.\n\n**Direct PTY master FD writes:**\nThe implementation previously used tty.ReadStream to write to the PTY. When writing directly to the PTY master FD with non-blocking mode, the I/O is async and queued through the libuv threadpool. This is relevant because writes via paste are separated by some natural delay which is more than enough time to process the write in the threadpool.\n\n**Natural typing simulation:**\nWhen data travels from PTY to the pseudoterminal master to the slave to the shell process, the shell receives the command as if it were typed on a keyboard.\n\nThe main difference appears to be that node-pty's `write()` method provides a higher-level abstraction for sending input to the PTY master, while direct FD writes operate at a lower level with different buffering and flow control characteristics. Maintaining an active session on a remote server allows for tasks such as concurrent log viewing, simultaneous file editing, and even simultaneous observation of ASCII movies. Multiplexers serve as optimal tools for facilitating these functionalities in such scenarios.\n\n## Output Capture\n\nEach window has its own scroll-back buffer, so that output is captured even when the window isn't actively displayed, and that history can be saved even when migrating to another computer. Additionally, recording actions for later review is a key feature available in terminal multiplexers.\n\n## Efficient Storage Approaches\n\nTerminal multiplexers support several efficient storage mechanisms:\n\n1. **Scroll-back buffers**: Built into each terminal window for capturing historical output\n2. **Session logging**: Screen sessions can be started with logging enabled (screen -L -S log_example), which creates a log file named 'screenlog.0' in the current directory.\n3. **State persistence**: Multiplexers save window states and settings even after disconnecting.\n\n## Resource Efficiency\n\nIf you're working on a constrained machine, having only a terminal open a multiplexer in it is easier on CPU/RAM consumption rather than multiple windows or tabs.\n\n## Key Multiplexer Tools\n\nPopular implementations include **tmux** and **GNU Screen**, both of which support efficient management of multiple concurrent terminal sessions with various output capture and storage options.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"3b8a7f7d-3fe2-46ba-8003-2bf643769f97","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:47:28.501Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"833dba4a-5332-4f64-9245-74a1e6ff830b","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

More specifically, SQLite is about 35% faster than direct disk I/O for both reads and writes of 10KB blobs, and SQLite can read and write smaller BLOBs (less than about 100KB in size) from its database faster than those same blobs can be read or written as separate files from the filesystem.\n\n## Storage Efficiency\n\nA single SQLite database containing 10-kilobyte BLOBs uses about 20% less disk space than storing BLOBs in individual files, because individual files are filled up to the next multiple of the filesystem block size, while BLOBs are more compactly packaged within an SQLite database.\n\n## Indexed Search Capabilities\n\nThe search results indicate that if performance problems arise in an SQLite application those problems can often be resolved by adding one or two CREATE INDEX statements to the schema or perhaps running ANALYZE one time and without having to touch a single line of application code. This makes SQLite well-suited for indexed searching compared to filesystem-based approaches.\n\n## Chunked Storage Option\n\nFor multigigabyte BLOBs that were built piecemeal, using many smaller BLOBs and concatenating them in application space on read out is feasible with SQLite, which just requires keeping a sequence number with each BLOB.\n\nThe research suggests SQLite is generally superior for storing and searching smaller data chunks (under 100KB-1MB), while filesystem storage becomes more efficient for very large individual items.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"16ee0c0e-032b-4781-9fb9-dc45aa4b5f3b","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:47:54.613Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"861799f9-9417-44c2-8eb4-a26639e91da8","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

User Guide\",\"url\":\"https://npg.dl.ac.uk/MIDAS/manual/ActiveTcl8.5.7.0.290198-html/expect4win/expect.html\"},{\"title\":\"expect\",\"url\":\"http://npg.dl.ac.uk/MIDAS/manual/ActiveTcl8.4.9.0-html/expect/expect.1.html\"},{\"title\":\"Expect - man pages section 1: User Commands\",\"url\":\"https://docs.oracle.com/cd/E88353_01/html/E37839/expect-1.html\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's how Expect's `spawn` command works internally with PTY pairs, fork, and exec:\n\n## Core Mechanism\n\nInternally, spawn forks, after which the spawned process has no way to communicate with the original Expect process except by communication via the spawn_id.\n\n## The Fork-Exec Pattern\n\nThe process follows the standard POSIX pattern: When using POSIX APIs, this has to be done by first forking and then having the child use the exec system call to replace itself with a different program.\n\n## File Descriptor Cleanup\n\nBetween the time a process is started and the new program is given control, the spawn functions can clean up the environment by closing file descriptors. The function will be called after the fork and before the exec.\n\n## PTY Handling\n\nWhen spawning with a PTY (the default), Expect manages both a master and slave side of the PTY pair. Some pty implementations may be confused by multiple readers and writers, even momentarily. Thus, it is safest to fork before spawning processes.\n\n## Error Handling\n\nIf program cannot be spawned successfully because exec(2) fails (e.g. when program doesn't exist), an error message will be returned by the next interact or expect command as if program had run and produced the error message as output. Errors that occur after the spawn functions fork (e.g., attempting to spawn a non-existent program) are written to the process's stderr, and will be read by the first expect.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"a59c8e37-3aad-4155-97ea-8e3e99a58bae","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:47:55.052Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"19c51de9-373e-4e53-b8ed-008bfff9ef27","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}
{"parentUuid":"a59c8e37-3aad-4155-97ea-8e3e99a58bae","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a8d2747051c8280b4","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_018TiztSPrucnw65Mpfm5BU2","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Now I have comprehensive data across all four research areas. Let me compile the findings.\n\n---\n\n# Terminal I/O Research Report: Capture, Storage, Replay, and Programmatic Input\n\n## 1. Smooth Programmatic Terminal Input\n\n### How expect, pexpect, and tmux send-keys work under the hood\n\nAll three tools share the same fundamental mechanism: **writing bytes to the master side of a PTY pair**.\n\n**Expect (Tcl)**: Calls `fork()`, creates a PTY pair (master + slave), attaches the slave to the child process's stdin/stdout/stderr, then `exec()`s the target program. The parent process holds the master fd. `send` writes bytes to the master fd. `expect` reads from the master fd and pattern-matches. The child process cannot distinguish these writes from a human typing.\n\n**Pexpect (Python)**: Pure Python reimplementation of the same pattern using the standard `pty` module. `spawn()` calls `pty.fork()` which handles the fork/openpty/setsid/ioctl dance. `send()` calls `os.write(self.child_fd, data)` on the master fd. The PTY line discipline handles CR/LF conversion, echo, and signal generation (e.g., Ctrl+C producing SIGINT).\n\n**tmux send-keys**: tmux already owns the PTY master for each pane. `send-keys` translates key names into byte sequences and writes them to that pane's PTY master fd via `bufferevent_write()`. The source is at [tmux/cmd-send-keys.c](https://github.com/tmux/tmux/blob/master/cmd-send-keys.c).\n\n### How Claude Code / Cursor / Copilot CLI inject input\n\n**Claude Code's Bash tool** does NOT use a PTY. It spawns `/bin/bash` via `subprocess.Popen` with stdin/stdout/stderr pipes, writes commands via `stdin.write()`, and reads stdout. This means it cannot handle interactive programs (no TTY allocation). There is an [open feature request](https://github.com/anthropics/claude-code/issues/9881) to add PTY support. Claude Code operates as a **non-interactive command executor**, not a terminal emulator.\n\n**VS Code / Roo Code / Kilo Code terminal integration** uses a different model: they create a VS Code terminal instance (which runs node-pty internally), send commands via the VS Code terminal API (`terminal.sendText()`), and capture output using **OSC 633 shell integration markers** (VS Code's extended variant of OSC 133). The `TerminalProcess` class watches for these markers to extract command output boundaries. Key markers: `OSC 633;A` (prompt start), `OSC 633;B` (prompt end), `OSC 633;C` (pre-execution), `OSC 633;D;exitcode` (execution finished), `OSC 633;E;commandline` (explicit command line).\n\n**Cursor** embeds its terminal using node-pty and injects input the same way node-pty does: writing to the PTY master fd. A [known bug](https://github.com/anthropics/claude-code/issues/34529) showed node-pty 1.1.0-beta42 failing to handle backpressure when large paste input was sent to a raw-mode PTY, causing freezes.\n\n### Best practices for programmatic PTY input\n\n**Writing to the PTY master fd is the canonical approach.** The child process's TTY line discipline treats these bytes identically to keyboard input. There is no need for \"simulation\" -- it IS real terminal input from the kernel's perspective.\n\nKey considerations:\n\n1. **TIOCSTI is deprecated.** Linux kernel >= 6.2 disabled `TIOCSTI` (the ioctl that injected characters into another terminal's input queue) by default due to security risks. The correct approach, as stated by the former TTY subsystem maintainer Alan Cox, is: \"create a pty/tty pair and use that.\" Your current node-pty architecture already does this correctly.\n\n2. **Bracketed paste mode**: When the shell/application has enabled bracketed paste mode (`\\e[?2004h`), pasted text is wrapped in `\\e[200~` ... `\\e[201~`. This tells the application \"this is pasted, not typed.\" For programmatic input that should look typed, do NOT wrap in these sequences. For bulk input (pasting file contents, multi-line commands), wrapping in bracketed paste markers is correct and prevents the shell from executing each line individually.\n\n3. **Raw mode handling**: When a program puts the terminal in raw mode (e.g., vim, less, fzf), every byte is delivered immediately without line-discipline processing. Writes to the master fd work normally, but you must send the exact escape sequences the application expects (e.g., arrow keys as `\\e[A`, not literal text).\n\n4. **Typing simulation**: To make input feel \"natural,\" insert small delays (10-50ms) between characters. However, this is purely cosmetic -- the kernel/shell cannot detect timing differences. The only scenario where timing matters is programs that distinguish \"fast paste\" from \"slow typing\" via bracketed paste mode or timing heuristics.\n\n5. **Flow control**: When sending large amounts of data, respect the PTY buffer size (typically 4096 bytes on Linux). node-pty handles this via libuv's threadpool for async writes, but backpressure can still cause issues (as seen in the Cursor bug).\n\n### Your current ANT implementation\n\nYour `pty-manager.ts` uses `node-pty` and writes to the PTY via `process.write(data)`, which is the correct approach. Your `terminal-monitor.ts` reads the headless terminal screen to detect Claude Code approval prompts -- this is a good polling-based approach for reading, but for injecting responses (e.g., approving a prompt), you would write the response bytes (like `y\\r`) to the PTY master.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Output Capture and Structured Storage\n\n### How Warp stores command blocks\n\nWarp's architecture, detailed in their [engineering blog](https://www.warp.dev/blog/the-data-structure-behind-terminals), uses a **multi-grid system**:\n\n- Each command block consists of **three separate grids**: prompt, command input, command output\n- The grid is a **circular buffer** storing rows in a flat vector (forked from Alacritty's model code)\n- A single grid would not work for blocks because terminal output from one command could overwrite another's grid cells via cursor movement escape sequences\n- Blocks are separated using **precmd/preexec shell hooks** (the shell-level equivalent of OSC 133)\n- The grid stores two integers for efficient scrolling: translating between screen-space and vector-space coordinates without moving data\n- On resize, the vector is rewritten to account for new column constraints (e.g., 10x10 becomes 20x5)\n\n### How VS Code captures and indexes command output\n\nVS Code's terminal uses **OSC 633 markers** (their extended version of OSC 133):\n- Shell integration scripts are injected automatically for bash, zsh, fish, and PowerShell\n- Output between `OSC 633;C` (command start) and `OSC 633;D` (command end) is captured\n- Output is stored in memory with a configurable line limit (default 500 lines, middle lines truncated when exceeded)\n- Command history is indexed by the terminal instance, allowing features like \"run recent command\" and \"go to recent directory\"\n- Known limitation: the system sometimes fails to capture output, requiring terminal restart\n\n### Storage format comparison\n\n| Format | Pros | Cons | Best For |\n|--------|------|------|----------|\n| **Raw ANSI** | Perfect fidelity, supports replay with colors/cursor | Large, hard to search, requires parser | Full session replay |\n| **Stripped text** | Searchable, compact | Loses formatting, cursor movement info | Full-text search, AI context |\n| **Asciicast v3** (asciinema) | Newline-delimited JSON, timing info, input/output/resize events, compressible to ~15% with zstd | Slightly larger than raw, requires player | Session recording, sharing, archival |\n| **script/typescript format** | Simple, built-in on all Unix, raw + timing file | Primitive, no structured metadata | Quick local recording |\n\n**Asciicast v3** (released September 2025 with the Rust rewrite of asciinema) is the strongest option for structured storage. Its format:\n- Header line: JSON with terminal size, env, timestamp, idle_time_limit, theme, XTVERSION\n- Event lines: `[delta_time, event_code, data]` where codes are: `o` (output), `i` (input), `m` (marker/breakpoint), `r` (resize as \"COLSxROWS\"), `x` (exit status)\n- Delta timestamps (v3 change from v2's absolute timestamps) make editing/splicing trivial\n- Supports live streaming in addition to recording\n\n### Efficient storage and search for large volumes\n\n**SQLite is the recommended storage backend** for terminal output chunks:\n- SQLite is [35% faster than filesystem](https://sqlite.org/fasterthanfs.html) for reads and writes of blobs under ~100KB\n- 20% less disk space than individual files (no filesystem block-size waste)\n- Built-in indexing for fast cursor-based retrieval\n- Your current ANT schema (`terminal_output_events` table with `session_id, chunk_index, data`) is already well-designed\n\n**Recommended schema enhancements:**\n\n```\nterminal_output_events:\n  session_id    TEXT\n  chunk_index   INTEGER\n  data          BLOB       -- raw ANSI bytes\n  timestamp     REAL       -- unix epoch with ms precision\n  event_type    TEXT       -- 'output', 'input', 'resize', 'marker'\n\ncommand_blocks:\n  id            TEXT PRIMARY KEY\n  session_id    TEXT\n  command       TEXT       -- stripped text of command\n  output_text   TEXT       -- stripped text for FTS\n  exit_code     INTEGER\n  cwd           TEXT\n  started_at    TEXT\n  completed_at  TEXT\n  duration_ms   INTEGER\n  detection     TEXT       -- 'osc133' or 'quiet'\n  start_chunk   INTEGER    -- FK to terminal_output_events\n  end_chunk     INTEGER    -- FK to terminal_output_events\n```\n\nAdd an **FTS5 virtual table** on `command_blocks.output_text` and `command_blocks.command` for full-text search across all sessions. This gives you Warp-like searchable command blocks without reinventing the storage layer.\n\nFor very high-volume scenarios (many concurrent sessions), consider **WAL mode** (write-ahead logging) in SQLite, which allows concurrent reads during writes.\n\n### Concurrent session capture\n\nYour current architecture (one `PtySession` per session in `pty-manager.ts`, each with its own `HeadlessTerminalWrapper` and `CommandTracker`) is already correct for concurrent capture. Each PTY's `onData` handler independently feeds its output to storage. The main optimization is **batching writes**: instead of inserting each chunk individually, batch inserts within a transaction every 100ms or so. Your current `resumeDebounce` pattern hints at this approach.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Command Boundary Detection\n\n### OSC 133 shell integration (the gold standard)\n\nThe OSC 133 protocol defines four semantic markers:\n- `\\e]133;A\\a` -- Prompt start (shell is idle, ready for input)\n- `\\e]133;B\\a` -- Prompt end / command line ready (text after this is the typed command)\n- `\\e]133;C\\a` -- Command execution start (user pressed Enter)\n- `\\e]133;D;{exitcode}\\a` -- Command execution finished\n\n**Your `command-tracker.ts` already implements this correctly**, including the fallback quiet-period detection. The implementation is solid.\n\n**Shell support status (2025-2026):**\n- **bash**: Supported via `PROMPT_COMMAND` injection (VS Code does this automatically; Ghostty, iTerm2, and WezTerm also inject scripts)\n- **zsh**: Native support being added upstream (workers mailing list patch from January 2025); currently supported via precmd/preexec hooks\n- **fish**: Well-supported via `fish_prompt` and `fish_preexec`/`fish_postexec` events\n- **PowerShell**: Supported via VS Code's shell integration script\n\n**Ghostty's improved approach**: Ghostty 1.3+ uses **region-based detection** rather than row-based, which is more accurate when output wraps across lines or the terminal is resized.\n\n### Heuristic approaches without shell integration\n\nYour 500ms quiet-period detection is the standard fallback. Other approaches:\n\n1. **Prompt regex matching**: Detect common prompt patterns (`$`, `#`, `%`, `>`) at line beginnings after output stops. Fragile but works for simple cases.\n2. **precmd/preexec hooks**: For zsh (`precmd`/`preexec` functions) and bash (`PROMPT_COMMAND` + `trap DEBUG`), you can inject hooks that emit custom markers WITHOUT requiring OSC 133 terminal support. Your app could inject `echo -ne \"\\e]133;C\\a\"` into the shell's preexec hook.\n3. **PS1 injection**: Modify the shell prompt to include a unique sentinel string that you can detect in output.\n4. **CWD detection via OSC 7**: You already parse `OSC 7` (`\\e]7;file://hostname/path\\a`) in `pty-manager.ts` for tracking the working directory. This fires on every prompt, so it's an implicit \"command finished\" signal.\n\n### Rich metadata capture (atuin/mcfly model)\n\n**Atuin** stores in SQLite (`~/.local/share/atuin/history.db`):\n- command text, timestamp, duration, exit code, CWD, hostname, session ID\n- Supports sync across machines via encrypted server\n- Has an [MCP server](https://skywork.ai/skypage/en/atuin-history-ai-command-line/1981676465415098368) for AI integration\n- [Claude Code integration](https://gist.github.com/joargp/6b698e1061b402670c0c2ce1a4cd4f6d) available via PostToolUse hook\n\n**McFly** uses a SQLite database with neural network ranking based on: command text, exit status, directory, command sequence context.\n\n**Recommendation for ANT**: Your `CommandEvent` interface already captures most of what atuin stores (command, exitCode, output, startedAt, completedAt, durationMs, cwd, detectionMethod). Consider adding: **hostname**, **shell type** (bash/zsh/fish), **git branch** (if in a git repo), and **terminal dimensions at time of execution**.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Modern Libraries and Frameworks\n\n### PTY libraries\n\n| Library | Language | Notes |\n|---------|----------|-------|\n| **node-pty** (microsoft) | C++/Node | Your current choice. Mature, used by VS Code. Requires node-gyp. Active maintenance. |\n| **@replit/ruspty** | Rust/Node (NAPI) | Thin Rust wrapper over PTY syscalls, exposed to Node via napi-rs (no node-gyp). Used in production at Replit. Actively maintained (last release May 2025). |\n| **portable-pty** | Rust | Part of WezTerm. Cross-platform (Unix PTY + Windows ConPTY). Trait-based API allows runtime backend selection. |\n| **bun-pty** | Rust/Bun | Uses portable-pty under the hood, exposed via Bun's FFI. |\n| **get-pty-output** | Rust/Node (NAPI) | Uses portable-pty (Unix) + conpty (Windows) with napi-rs bindings. Avoids node-gyp entirely. |\n\n**Recommendation**: **@replit/ruspty** is the strongest node-pty replacement candidate. It eliminates the node-gyp build dependency (the single most painful aspect of node-pty), uses napi-rs for clean Rust-to-Node bindings, and is battle-tested at Replit's scale. However, node-pty remains the most feature-complete and well-documented option. A migration should be evaluated against your specific pain points with node-pty.\n\n### WebAssembly-based terminal approaches\n\n**ghostty-web** ([github.com/coder/ghostty-web](https://github.com/coder/ghostty-web)) is the most significant development in this space:\n- Ghostty's VT100 parser (written in Zig) compiled to ~400KB WASM\n- Drop-in replacement for xterm.js (compatible API: just change the import)\n- Same battle-tested emulation as the native Ghostty terminal app\n- Created by Coder (the company behind code-server) for their \"Mux\" product\n- VS Code extension already exists that replaces xterm.js with ghostty-web\n- JupyterLab has an [open issue](https://github.com/jupyterlab/jupyterlab/issues/17702) to adopt it\n- xterm.js itself has an [issue exploring adoption](https://github.com/xtermjs/xterm.js/issues/5686) of libghostty\n\nOther WASM terminal options:\n- **wasm-webterm**: xterm.js addon for running WASM binaries (WASI + Emscripten)\n- **xterm-pty**: Bridge connecting xterm.js to Emscripten-compiled programs\n- **Wasmer SDK**: Runs bash + Unix utilities in-browser via WASIX\n\n**Recommendation for ANT**: ghostty-web is worth evaluating as a future xterm.js replacement. The API compatibility means migration risk is low. However, xterm.js remains more mature and has a larger ecosystem of addons (serialize, search, webgl renderer, etc.). Wait for ghostty-web to stabilize further before migrating a production app.\n\n### AI-agent terminal interaction frameworks\n\nThe landscape is converging on a clear pattern: **spawn a sandboxed environment with PTY access, write commands to the PTY master, read output, repeat.**\n\nKey frameworks and infrastructure:\n- **E2B** (e2b.dev): Cloud sandboxes with PTY access, designed for AI agents\n- **Daytona**: Sub-90ms sandbox provisioning, full PTY support, raised $24M Series A (Feb 2026)\n- **Docker Sandboxes**: Experimental Docker feature specifically for AI agent isolation\n- **OpenAI Agents SDK**: Lightweight Python framework (March 2025) with terminal agent support\n- **AgentBay**: Hybrid interaction sandbox for human-AI intervention in agentic systems\n\nA relevant research paper, [\"Terminal Is All You Need\"](https://arxiv.org/html/2603.10664) (March 2026), argues that terminal-based interaction is the optimal interface for human-AI agent collaboration. Another paper, [\"Terminal Agents Suffice for Enterprise Automation\"](https://arxiv.org/html/2604.00073) (April 2026), demonstrates that LLMs given full Ubuntu terminal access can automate enterprise workflows effectively.\n\nThere is no single \"framework for AI-agent terminal interaction\" yet -- the pattern is still: PTY library + shell integration + output capture + LLM loop. ANT is actually building toward this pattern already.\n\n---\n\n## Concrete Recommendations for ANT\n\n1. **Keep node-pty for now**, but track @replit/ruspty as a future migration target to eliminate node-gyp pain.\n\n2. **Invest in OSC 133 shell integration injection**. On session creation, inject shell-specific hooks (precmd/preexec for zsh, PROMPT_COMMAND + trap DEBUG for bash) that emit OSC 133 markers. This upgrades your command boundary detection from \"quiet period heuristic\" to \"exact boundaries with exit codes\" without requiring the user to configure anything.\n\n3. **Add a `command_blocks` table** with FTS5 indexing on command text and stripped output. This enables Warp-like searchable command history across all sessions.\n\n4. **Consider adopting asciicast v3 format** for session recording/export. The delta-timestamp NDJSON format is simple to generate from your existing output stream and enables replay via the asciinema player.\n\n5. **Evaluate ghostty-web** as a future xterm.js replacement for the browser-side terminal. The API is compatible, the emulation is higher quality, and the WASM bundle is smaller.\n\n6. **For programmatic input, your current approach is correct**: write bytes to the PTY master via node-pty's `write()`. For approval automation (terminal-monitor.ts), wrap multi-line input in bracketed paste markers (`\\e[200~...\\e[201~`) when appropriate, and send bare bytes for single-key responses.\n\n---\n\nSources:\n- [Pexpect Documentation](https://pexpect.readthedocs.io/en/stable/overview.html)\n- [tmux send-keys source](https://github.com/tmux/tmux/blob/master/cmd-send-keys.c)\n- [Claude Code Bash Tool Docs](https://platform.claude.com/docs/en/agents-and-tools/tool-use/bash-tool)\n- [Claude Code PTY Feature Request](https://github.com/anthropics/claude-code/issues/9881)\n- [Warp: The Data Structure Behind Terminals](https://www.warp.dev/blog/the-data-structure-behind-terminals)\n- [Warp: How Warp Works](https://www.warp.dev/blog/how-warp-works)\n- [VS Code Terminal Shell Integration](https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/terminal/shell-integration)\n- [Roo Code Terminal Shell Integration](https://docs.roocode.com/features/shell-integration)\n- [Kilo Code Terminal
